    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<span id="freeTextContainer3587386527552778965">El "Romancero gitano" difunde el nombre de su autor por todo el ámbito de la literatura de nuestra lengua. Aclara el poeta que "aunque se llama gitano, es el poema de Andalucía porque el gitano es lo más elevado, lo más profundo, lo más aristocrático, lo más representativo de su modo y el que guarda el ascua, la sangre y el alfabeto de la verdad andaluza y universal".</span>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_2110914730" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2110914730" />
    <a title="Brina" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2933855-brina"><img alt="Brina" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601675085p2/2933855.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2110914730?book_show_action=true">Sep 01, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brina" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brina" href="/user/show/2933855-brina">Brina</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781907587085" href="/book/show/12697862-gypsy-ballads">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2933855-brina?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2933855-brina?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2110914730" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3400302604283031954">Over the course of 2017, I have read a number of poetry collections both by American born and foreign poets. While I find myself preferring contemporary poetry, I do respect classic collections and have some favorite poets who I prefer. As this is Hispanic Heritage Month, I decided to step out of my comfort zone and read the work of noted poet laureate <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/44150.Federico_Garc_a_Lorca' title='Federico García Lorca' rel='nofollow noopener'>Federico García Lorca</a>. I had read his play <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/22991.Blood_Wedding_and_Yerma_' title='Blood Wedding and Yerma  by Federico García Lorca' rel='nofollow noopener'>Blood Wedding and Yerma</a> in a Spanish literature course while in college and came to realize the depth </span>
  <span id="freeText3400302604283031954" style="display:none">Over the course of 2017, I have read a number of poetry collections both by American born and foreign poets. While I find myself preferring contemporary poetry, I do respect classic collections and have some favorite poets who I prefer. As this is Hispanic Heritage Month, I decided to step out of my comfort zone and read the work of noted poet laureate <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/44150.Federico_Garc_a_Lorca" title="Federico García Lorca" rel="nofollow noopener">Federico García Lorca</a>. I had read his play <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/22991.Blood_Wedding_and_Yerma_" title="Blood Wedding and Yerma  by Federico García Lorca" rel="nofollow noopener">Blood Wedding and Yerma</a> in a Spanish literature course while in college and came to realize the depth of his work; yet, I had never been exposed to his brilliant poetry. While my Hispanic Heritage Month selections are primarily centered on the Latin American experience, I decided upon Garcia Lorca's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12697862.Gypsy_Ballads" title="Gypsy Ballads by Federico García Lorca" rel="nofollow noopener">Gypsy Ballads</a> to show appreciation to the Spanish origins of Hispanic culture.<br /><br />Frederico Garcia Lorca is regarded as the most popular of Spanish poets and playwrights. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12697862.Gypsy_Ballads" title="Gypsy Ballads by Federico García Lorca" rel="nofollow noopener">Gypsy Ballads</a> is considered his best selling work, and many feel it is one of the top poetry collections of all time. Many of his poems focus on the depth of the human experience, which is a main reason I read poetry-- to feel the gamut of emotions over the course of a few moving stanzas. Themes experienced in Garcia Lorca's work include desire, death, as well as human longing and social repression. The latter theme propelled Franco's supporters to kill the poet at the beginning of the Spanish Civil War as a figurehead of the repressed people. I admit, that any pseudo-analysis I give on these ballads will not do justice to the laureate's work. For this reason, I chose to read a translated version by Jane Duran and Gloria Garcia Lorca, the poet's niece, which also includes commentary by noted translator Christopher Maurer as well as an essay by Garcia Lorca himself. Because of the depth of the poems and my love of history, I was actually more intrigued by the essays at the end than some of the harder to grasp albeit beautiful poetry.<br /><br />The Gypsy Ballads speak to the myth of the people of Andalusia in the south of Spain. In addition to the death already permeating Spain leading up to the Guerra Civil, the ballads feature the patchwork of people that make up Spain's southern province, including the shared history of Christians, Muslims, and Jews. The poetry contains many biblical images which speak to each of these people. In "The Unfaithful Wife," we see, according to Lorca, Soledad Montoya whose pain is emblematic of the Andalusian people. This poem, as much as any in the collection, speaks of the gypsies, as Lorca writes so eloquently, "As a true gypsy. I gave her a large sewing basket of straw-yellow satin, and I did not want to fall in love because, having a husband, she told me she was a maiden when I took her to the river." Other poems include biblical references to archangels Michael, Rafael, and Gabriel. Reading these ballads over the new year gave them an added layer of religiosity that added to the prestige and beauty of these works.<br /><br />The final poem in the collection Thamar and Amnon reflects on the Jewish and gypsy history of the region. Garcia Lorca notes that Andalusians refer to Tamar as "altas mares" (high seas). The wording in this ballad appears almost mystical in proportion as it speaks of the gypsy culture of Cordoba and Granada. While reading it through, I felt as though the history of hidden Jewish people flowed from the pages. Some highlights include Tamar's dream with imagery of birds, palm trees, and doves, all evoking biblical references. Another stanza alludes to King David's harp as the poet writes, "Furious violator, Amnon flees on his horse. Black men point arrows at him from the walls and watchtowers. And when the four hooves were four echoes, David, with his scissors, cut the strings of his harp." As the conclusion of this moving collection, I thought this ballad summarized the shared religious history of southern Spain, where three religions lived in relative harmony for seven centuries. Unfortunately, with the rise of fascism, minority people were oppressed, with Garcia Lorca's becoming a symbol for gypsies and the other human suffering which he wrote so eloquently about.<br /><br />Reading the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12697862.Gypsy_Ballads" title="Gypsy Ballads by Federico García Lorca" rel="nofollow noopener">Gypsy Ballads</a> only as poetry collection does not do justice to the brilliance of Spanish poet laureate <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/44150.Federico_Garc_a_Lorca" title="Federico García Lorca" rel="nofollow noopener">Federico García Lorca</a>. Reading it combined as art and a history of the Andalusian region of Spain speaks to the beauty of Garcia Lorca's work. In some cases it took me many times to begin to grasp the meaning of the words written on these pages. I did appreciate the essay by the poet at the end of the collection which assisted in bringing meaning to the words which he so beautifully wrote during his brief life. I can see why <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12697862.Gypsy_Ballads" title="Gypsy Ballads by Federico García Lorca" rel="nofollow noopener">Gypsy Ballads</a> is one of the top selling poetry collections of all time as I feel the beauty of long, shared multicultural history flow from its pages.<br /><br />4.5 stars</span>
  <a data-text-id="3400302604283031954" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer3127393658761944509">Sex, violence, rape and death. This is stuff poems are made of. It is supposed to be deeply routed in Spanish culture. Lorca used to sing them when he was still in process of writing them in front of audience, and they left such a deep impact on people that they were quoted by other writers and inspired other works even before they were published.   <span id="freeText3127393658761944509" style="display:none">Sex, violence, rape and death. This is stuff poems are made of. It is supposed to be deeply routed in Spanish culture. Lorca used to sing them when he was still in process of writing them in front of audience, and they left such a deep impact on people that they were quoted by other writers and inspired other works even before they were published. I guess they wells on the habbit people had world over (before Guttenberg) of making songs on the events that leave deep impact on imagination of people to help remembering events in future. Except in this case they are fictional events. <br /><br />These poems are often about battle between opposities - desire and purity, life and death. Some of them take form of conversations between characters - and some of the characters are gods (creating <i>new</i> mythic tales) There is lots of symbolism too - which you start being able to understand as you read on. A third reason to want to learn Spanish after Neruda and Marquez.</span>

<div id="review_2125194851" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2125194851" />
    <a title="B. P. Rinehart" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4635205-b-p-rinehart"><img alt="B. P. Rinehart" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1347740735p2/4635205.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2125194851?book_show_action=true">Nov 27, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="B. P. Rinehart" class="user" itemprop="url" name="B. P. Rinehart" href="/user/show/4635205-b-p-rinehart">B. P. Rinehart</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook " href="/book/show/36247670-gypsy-ballads">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4635205-b-p-rinehart?shelf=poetry-stuff">poetry-stuff</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2125194851" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17241331611798521632">"<i>Soledad of my sorrows,<br/>horse that takes its head,<br/>you will end up in the sea<br/>where the waves will swallow you.</i>" - stanza from "Ballad of Deep Sorrow"<br/><br/><br/>So I decided to read the national poet of Spain: <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/44150.Federico_Garc_a_Lorca' title='Federico García Lorca' rel='nofollow noopener'>Federico García Lorca</a>. This volume is said to be his most celebrated poetry. In a way, if you are a fan of the things Lorca was a fan of this makes sense. This volume is called <u>Romancero gitano</u> in Spanish which is as acurate as calling it a ballad. The poems in this collection deal with sex and death th</span>
  <span id="freeText17241331611798521632" style="display:none">"<i>Soledad of my sorrows,<br />horse that takes its head,<br />you will end up in the sea<br />where the waves will swallow you.</i>" - stanza from "Ballad of Deep Sorrow"<br /><br /><br />So I decided to read the national poet of Spain: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/44150.Federico_Garc_a_Lorca" title="Federico García Lorca" rel="nofollow noopener">Federico García Lorca</a>. This volume is said to be his most celebrated poetry. In a way, if you are a fan of the things Lorca was a fan of this makes sense. This volume is called <u>Romancero gitano</u> in Spanish which is as acurate as calling it a ballad. The poems in this collection deal with sex and death through the stereotypical-image of the Roma people in Spain. Lorca himself admitted that these poems were not to be taken as case history, but it still perpetuated the stereotypical image of these people anyway. On the other hand, Lorca was almost single-handedly responsible for making the culture of the Roma accepted as being a part of Spanish culture at all. They went from being regarded as lower than the Jews in 1920s Europe to being like African-Americans in 1920s USA (we like your music, but not you). Lorca's promotion of a 1922 festival of Flamenco music introduced the genre (which had been around since the late-Middle Ages) not just to non-Andalusian Spain, but the rest of the world. Legacies are complicated.<br /><br />This particular edition I wanted to read because it was translated by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/36910.Langston_Hughes" title="Langston Hughes" rel="nofollow noopener">Langston Hughes</a>. Hughes is mostly known for his work as a fiction writer, but he has a very unexplored history as a translator and a facilitator of translation between Spanish and English language writers. He was also a part of the same political circles as Lorca and was supposed to have met with Lorca in Madrid to translate this book. Unfortunately, Lorca was killed by right-wing Civil Guards loyal to Francisco Franco the week before Hughes arrived. This makes the books use of the Civil Guards as a menacing dangerous group justified (some of these poems almost foreshadow his death). <br /><br />These poems where hit-and-miss for me. While I liked the simple imagery of the words...I'm just not that big a fan of these make-believed fantasy gypsies that are in this poetry. The structual ideas seemed to be clashing with the subject matter. Don't get me wrong, I love Flamenco, but I am not that keen on the Romanticism of minorities as being exocticzed or fetishsized.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17241331611798521632" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1822025488" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1822025488" />
    <a title="Roy Lotz" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14046996-roy-lotz"><img alt="Roy Lotz" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1443324405p2/14046996.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1822025488?book_show_action=true">Nov 08, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Roy Lotz" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Roy Lotz" href="/user/show/14046996-roy-lotz">Roy Lotz</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14046996-roy-lotz?shelf=no-hablo-ingl%C3%A9s">no-hablo-inglés</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14046996-roy-lotz?shelf=hispanophilia">hispanophilia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14046996-roy-lotz?shelf=best-words-best-order">best-words-best-order</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1822025488" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3919643232038606305"><i>Romancero gitano</i> is Federico García Lorca’s most popular poetry collection. It is a slim book, consisting of 18 poems, most of them no longer than three pages. The poems treat of Lorca’s native Andalusia—with religious processions, saint days, gypsy singers, civil guards patrolling at night. The collection is perhaps most famous for its iconic <i>“Romance sonámbulo,”</i> which contains Lorca’s most famous lines, ones which every Spaniard knows instinctively: <i>“Verde que te quiero verde”</i>—made into a well</span>
  <span id="freeText3919643232038606305" style="display:none"><i>Romancero gitano</i> is Federico García Lorca’s most popular poetry collection. It is a slim book, consisting of 18 poems, most of them no longer than three pages. The poems treat of Lorca’s native Andalusia—with religious processions, saint days, gypsy singers, civil guards patrolling at night. The collection is perhaps most famous for its iconic <i>“Romance sonámbulo,”</i> which contains Lorca’s most famous lines, ones which every Spaniard knows instinctively: <i>“Verde que te quiero verde”</i>—made into a well-known song by the band Manzanita.<br /><br />(Incidentally, I don’t know how to properly translate that line: “Green I want you green,” or “Green I love you green.” And in any case, I don’t know what it is supposed to mean. But it sounds great in Spanish.)<br /><br />In style the poems are quite different from Lorca’s <i>Poeta en Nueva York</i>. For one, these poems follow a meter and a rhyme scheme; the word <i>“romancero”</i> means a collection of ballads, and indeed these poems are easy to sing. What is more, these poems have none of the surrealistic horror of the New York poems; instead they romanticize a bucolic life under the Mediterranean sun. For my part, I quite enjoyed the poems for their hypnotizing rhythm, but very little has stuck with me in the way that Lorca’s New York poems have.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3919643232038606305" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_2662235504" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2662235504" />
    <a title="Nathan Albright" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/782114-nathan-albright"><img alt="Nathan Albright" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1200342559p2/782114.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2662235504?book_show_action=true">Jan 07, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nathan Albright" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nathan Albright" href="/user/show/782114-nathan-albright">Nathan Albright</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780241371879" href="/book/show/41081374-gypsy-ballads">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/782114-nathan-albright?shelf=challenge-2019">challenge-2019</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2662235504" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3732551739215215514">Rarely is a book more pregnant with ominous hints about an author's destiny than this book is.  Lorca, with whom I have been familiar with since high school when my Spanish teacher assigned his play "La Casa De Bernalda Alba" to our class, was certainly far more sympathetic to the gypsies than most people of his time (or ours) are, and this particular short book is full of poems, and meditations on poems, that give the sensitive reader a sense of what was lost when Lorca was put to death by the </span>
  <span id="freeText3732551739215215514" style="display:none">Rarely is a book more pregnant with ominous hints about an author's destiny than this book is.  Lorca, with whom I have been familiar with since high school when my Spanish teacher assigned his play "La Casa De Bernalda Alba" to our class, was certainly far more sympathetic to the gypsies than most people of his time (or ours) are, and this particular short book is full of poems, and meditations on poems, that give the sensitive reader a sense of what was lost when Lorca was put to death by the fascists at the beginning of the Spanish Civil War.  It is precisely unclear at this point what led him to be targeted, but as a thoughtful writer with a strong interest in various sorts of inappropriate sexuality (of which plenty can be found in these pages) and with a strong degree of personal disagreement with authoritarian government or policing, it is pretty clear that he was marked as an early enemy of the regime.  The fact that his family had to live in exile and that he was friends with a few people who were open supporters of Franco's regime, some very successful, suggests that he was not an ugly partisan but rather someone with a very motley group of friends and associates, and even lovers, as the case may be.<br /><br />While most of this more than 200 page book contains the ballads themselves, there is other worthwhile material here for those who are not as well versed (as I am not) with the life and career of Lorca.  The book begins with acknowledgements, then moves on to an introduction to the translations, which include a fair bit of transliteration of words that are either difficult to translate or used in a multi-layered way by Lorca.  After this comes a discussion about the gypsy ballads as a genre, the dedications, and the shades of green contained in them.  After the poems themelseves there are notes on the poems, a lecture that the poet himself had given on them, and notes on the various contributors to the books.  As far as the poems themselves are concerned, they include biblical references (Thamar and Amnón), references to deviant sexuality of some kind (Preciosa and the Air deals with the threat of rape, The Unfaithful wife with adultery, and so on), as well as meditations on death and the threat of the Spanish Civil Guard.  The poems are given in a diglot fashion with Spanish on the left page and English on the right.<br /><br />Overall, the poems are quite beautiful and deeply tragic.  While some of the themes discussed are conventional, Lorca is a sensitive poet and has a great deal of skill that comes through both in his native Andalucian Spanish and in English translation.  The author's poetry captures a rich and complex picture of hidden and perverse longings, cultures living close to each other but with the threat of violence in order to enforce some sort of conformity, as well as the power of death and love.  Lorca himself was sensitive to these matters, and while he was a good friend to a diverse group of people, his love affairs tended to end up somewhat unhappy, as is often the case with poets.  These poems dwell on unhappiness, with people dying of love or being chased by unwanted amorous partners or being deceived by those they are in love with.  Perhaps the author uses the title of gypsy ballads as a way of distancing these poems from his own life and his own preoccupations, but he would have been wiser to have reflected more seriously on the premonitions these books show about his own early and unfortunate fate.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3732551739215215514" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2344790464" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2344790464" />
    <a title="Sam" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1954710-sam"><img alt="Sam" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1489370002p2/1954710.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2344790464?book_show_action=true">Mar 31, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sam" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sam" href="/user/show/1954710-sam">Sam</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9788420633848" href="/book/show/2914229-romancero-gitano">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1954710-sam?shelf=fun-lit">fun-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1954710-sam?shelf=spanish">spanish</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1954710-sam?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2344790464" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10190025564389758226">This is the first complete book of Lorca's poetry that I have read, and I am certainly going to continue into his other collections. <br/><br/>The edition is good as well--very portable, but the print isn't too small. The introduction and final critical portion are very focused on the chronology in which these poems were written, which isn't of great interest to me but I can certainly understand the desire to trace out the poet's trajectory. Since he is one of the exemplary Spanish poets, who was very muc</span>
  <span id="freeText10190025564389758226" style="display:none">This is the first complete book of Lorca's poetry that I have read, and I am certainly going to continue into his other collections. <br /><br />The edition is good as well--very portable, but the print isn't too small. The introduction and final critical portion are very focused on the chronology in which these poems were written, which isn't of great interest to me but I can certainly understand the desire to trace out the poet's trajectory. Since he is one of the exemplary Spanish poets, who was very much focused on capturing what was beautiful about Spain, I can see why people would want to trace his travels throughout it and the development of his ideas about what was great in his homeland. The editor, Mario Hernández, also includes some other poems written around the same time as the main work, and that will help give people a taste of Lorca's other work (the extra poems are mostly taken from his theater). Perhaps the editorial decision was to include transcripts or reproductions of several presentations/interviews with the author. These give us a picture of what the young Lorca was like in the moment that Romancero gitano was published. <br /><br />The poems themselves are obviously the stars here. They paint Andalucía like something out of a dream--lots of mountains, wind, dark nights, passion, and violence that springs out of nowhere. Lots of smells and plants specific to areas like Granada and Mérida. I think my favorite poem of the whole bunch isn't set in the south, but in Castilla-La Mancha: "Reyerta." Set in antiquity, some Romans are dispatched to handle a violent situation, and it has what I think is one of my favorite series of lines in Spanish poetry: "La tarde, loca de higueras / y de rumores calientes, / cae desmayada en los muslos / heridos de los jinetes" (53). I don't know--that just blows me away. <br /><br />There are several other poems in the collection, ballads, that are more widely anthologized, like "Romance de la luna, luna," and those are obviously great as well. They often point to how quickly violence can erupt in these places, whether between jealous gypsies ("Muerte de Antoñito Camborio") or from the police ("Romance de la Guardia Civil española"). This (self-)destruction is part of the tragedy of the whole work. It's always in mourning or anticipating death (Lorca mentions this in one of the interviews included in the edition). <br /><br />This collection really transports you to Spain, but it's not filled with specific details about certain towns or cities. Some are mentioned, but, again, they're in a dream-like state. It more just gives you a general feeling of Spain.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10190025564389758226" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1408811542" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1408811542" />
    <a title="David Miller" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7976644-david-miller"><img alt="David Miller" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1360321966p2/7976644.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1408811542?book_show_action=true">Oct 04, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Miller" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Miller" href="/user/show/7976644-david-miller">David Miller</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781907587085" href="/book/show/12697862-gypsy-ballads">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1408811542" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer360201485288349487">These are beautiful poems that truly must be read in Spanish to be properly appreciated. I don't say that to sound pretentious, as I found myself constantly referring to the English translations when confronted with rare verbs, names of flowers and plants, and poetic inversions of word order. But reading the original Spanish, one becomes aware of a powerful balladic rhythm that simply isn't there in the English. It would seem the translators aimed primarily at preserving García Lorca's fantastic</span>
  <span id="freeText360201485288349487" style="display:none">These are beautiful poems that truly must be read in Spanish to be properly appreciated. I don't say that to sound pretentious, as I found myself constantly referring to the English translations when confronted with rare verbs, names of flowers and plants, and poetic inversions of word order. But reading the original Spanish, one becomes aware of a powerful balladic rhythm that simply isn't there in the English. It would seem the translators aimed primarily at preserving García Lorca's fantastic imagery and in remaining comprehensible. In any event, it's a truism that poems in translation rarely measure up to their originals.<br /><br />Steeped in death, violence, and myth, but also teeming with life and the beauty of nature and humanity, a kind of story of Lorca's native Andalucía emerges from this collection. That story is mysterious, sometimes impressionistic, and often nightmarish. The final impression is of a country that is alive, with all the joy and suffering that entails.<br /><br />I thought about giving this five stars. When I decided not to, it all came down to a fundamental issue I took with the book, namely its relationship with Roma people. It is clear from the poems and the lecture included at the end of the book that Lorca admired and sympathized with the Roma. Unfortunately, it appears to me that he also idolized them somewhat inordinately, even as he assumes some of his readers to have put him and this book on a pedestal. It looks to me like this book is part of the tradition of romanticizing Roma culture in Europe and America, by people who would appropriate their imagined "nomad" aesthetics while condemning real Roma people as dirty or criminal, if they should ever think of them at all. <br /><br />I don't think Lorca felt that way about them, but I've met plenty of people who do and I don't think book titles like <i>Gypsy Ballads</i> do much to dissuade them. In point of fact, "gypsy" is widely considered a racial slur in English by the Roma. I can't speak to the same status of the Spanish term "gitano", but in any case it is an exonym applied to people who have rarely benefited from being defined by others.<br /><br />All that being said, <i>Romancero Gitano</i> is a fabulous book of poetry. I hope that, for all the beauty it depicts in the culture and heritage of southern Spain, it will continue to be read by open minds, especially for the sake of its eponymous subjects.</span>
  <a data-text-id="360201485288349487" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_654786656" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/654786656" />
    <a title="David" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7312219-david"><img alt="David" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1325542079p2/7312219.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/654786656?book_show_action=true">Jun 29, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David" href="/user/show/7312219-david">David</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780856684913" href="/book/show/782581.Gypsy_Ballads">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7312219-david?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer654786656" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4987267038711011460">There were many poems that didn't engage me very much (especially the ones whose detailed Christian references went over my head), but they were balanced with a number of poems that are among the best I have ever read (not that I've read a ton of poetry, but still), so I'm going to give Gypsy Ballads a four overall. I think Lorca's best poems are in some ways very simple (using a lexicon of very common and recognizable symbols), yet he ties his words in such a fascinating way that makes his poet</span>
  <span id="freeText4987267038711011460" style="display:none">There were many poems that didn't engage me very much (especially the ones whose detailed Christian references went over my head), but they were balanced with a number of poems that are among the best I have ever read (not that I've read a ton of poetry, but still), so I'm going to give Gypsy Ballads a four overall. I think Lorca's best poems are in some ways very simple (using a lexicon of very common and recognizable symbols), yet he ties his words in such a fascinating way that makes his poetry soar with passion. <br /><br />As a collection of only 18 poems, the book's thematic cohesion is remarkable. I think Lorca made an ingenious effort to humanize and celebrate the Gypsies/Romani of his native Andalucia by co-opting their stereotyped "mystic" quality into something passionate and profound. And don't let the word "ballad" fool you - these poems are filled with as much anguish and violence as they are with love.<br /><br />If you have even mediocre Spanish language skills, please read this in Spanish, even if you have to use a dictionary and a translation as stepping stones (like I did). You can still get a glance at Lorca's skills through translation, but the full scope of Lorca's power (even more so than most translated authors, I think) is unfortunately only available in its original language.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4987267038711011460" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

That often didn't turn out to be the case. After finishing the ~18 ballads, I went onto the translator or editor's extensive critical examination that made up 70% of the book, during which I ran out of steam. I could see re-reading this work much more slowly, and referring to the critical notes before, during and after the poem, but I'm not in a patient</span>
  <span id="freeText9795984293435280410" style="display:none">I went in assuming I could appreciate this without a lot of background on Andalusian saints, figures of ancient Rome, and the Spanish Civil War. That often didn't turn out to be the case. After finishing the ~18 ballads, I went onto the translator or editor's extensive critical examination that made up 70% of the book, during which I ran out of steam. I could see re-reading this work much more slowly, and referring to the critical notes before, during and after the poem, but I'm not in a patient mindset at this time, nor am I generally, and I am ready to go back to prose for now..<br /><br />The bilingual edition was great though, and at a glance Carl Cobb's translation seemed more succinct and natural than others beside it in the library.<br /><br />So 3 stars for myself basically, and _ stars for Romancero Gitano.<br /><br />Passages I liked:<br />Huye, luna, luna, luna<br />Si vinieran los gitanos,<br />harían con tú coranzón <br />collares y anillos blancos<br />--<br />Su luna de pergamino<br />Preciosa tocando viene<br />por un anfibio sendero<br />de cristales y laureles<br />--<br />Y mientras cuenta, llorando,<br />su aventura a aquella gente<br />en las tejas de pizarra<br />el viento, furioso, muerde.<br />--<br />Vuelan en la araña gris,<br />siete pájaros del prisma.<br />La iglesia gruñe a lo lejos<br />como un oso panza arriba.</span>

<div id="review_1858046509" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1858046509" />

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1858046509?book_show_action=true">Jan 05, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Thomas" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Thomas" href="/user/show/52482799-thomas">Thomas</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1858046509" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8698228879889345748">A short collection of atmospheric, lyrical poems taking on the themes and settings of Andalucia and gitano culture. The poems are not simple but follow simple structures and powerful repetition like popular song, oral storytelling and mythology. Their short lines and regular rhymes create a fast, heroic pace and the stories they tell are full of travel, romance and violence. Lorca conjures semi-mystical landscapes, swirling with all four elements and bathed in moon and starlight. The flora and f</span>
  <span id="freeText8698228879889345748" style="display:none">A short collection of atmospheric, lyrical poems taking on the themes and settings of Andalucia and gitano culture. The poems are not simple but follow simple structures and powerful repetition like popular song, oral storytelling and mythology. Their short lines and regular rhymes create a fast, heroic pace and the stories they tell are full of travel, romance and violence. Lorca conjures semi-mystical landscapes, swirling with all four elements and bathed in moon and starlight. The flora and fauna of the gitano world and the landscapes of Andalucia characterise his poems with an almost Spanish Wild West feeling, full of daring rescues, duels, martyrs and doomed lovers. Read out loud these poems have a wonderful, rolling lyricism and a epic sense of drama, interjected with exclamations and cries of hope and despair. 6</span>
  <a data-text-id="8698228879889345748" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText6307901351857030920" style="display:none"><br /> Romancero Gitano(Gypsy Ballads) a collection of poetry by Spanish playwright and poet Federico Garcia Lorca published in 1928 and composed of 18 ballads focusing on gypsy life using subjects like the night the moon death and the sky to convey the author's message.         <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1986582568" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2782140032536238452">The "Firefly" of 20th century poetry--a brief flare whose fiery trail across the sky leaves you running for the horizon to stay caught up with it. Sadly, it burns out and you're left wondering, "Why didn't I get more? We deserve more!". Too bad for you, and probably better that it ended when it did. Supreme poetry.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('+wY3LO0MtEVaXAE4hsfZGEauOBqyamB5+15biFWi2FSKyZW1lpG141zgOefYLW3Ei7Up8Mo9bM34GakJfVmBAg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('fJVlYKphLHO0rGuSUg918dAoRfCLzsI7XQzT1GA2y8MNWsf50fwt1bIQU00M5cEtHTNUGvOZzo9eSyFVSM2SlQ==')})
  }
//]]>

Es muy celebre el primer parrafo: “Platero es pequeño, peludo, suave; tan blando por fuera, que se diría todo de algodón, que no lleva hue<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText7483047631863016376\" style=\"display:none\">Platero y yo es una narración de Juan Ramón Jiménez que recrea poéticamente la vida y muerte del burro Platero. Es muy celebre el primer parrafo: “Platero es pequeño, peludo, suave; tan blando por fuera, que se diría todo de algodón, que no lleva huesos. Sólo los espejos de azabache de sus ojos son duros cual dos escarabajos de cristal negro. Lo dejo suelto y se va al prado y acaricia tibiamente, rozándolas apenas, las florecillas rosas, celestes y gualdas... Lo llamo dulcemente: ¿Platero?, y viene a mi con un trotecillo alegre, que parece que se ríe, en no sé que cascabeleo ideal...”\n\nLa primera edición se publico en 1914 (Ediciones De La Lectura) y en 1917 se publicó la edición completa, compuesta por 138 capítulos (Editorial Calleja, Madrid). Quedaba claro que era un texto adulto, aunque por su sencillez y transparencia se adecuara perfectamente a la imaginación y al gusto de los niños. Algunos capítulos encerraban una cierta critica social, revelando una dimensión del autor que muchos tardaron en advertir. El propio Juan Ramón Jiménez, en un prologuillo a la edición aclaraba: “Yo nunca he escrito ni escribiré nada para niños porque creo que el niño puede leer los libros que lee el hombre, con determinadas excepciones que a todos se le ocurren.”\n\nEl poeta tenía la intención de ampliar el texto hasta los 190 capítulos; de hecho, existen tres adicionales, escritos en la década de 1920. Juan Ramón Jiménez planeó también una segunda parte, denominada Otra vida de Platero, de la que incluso esbozó algunos títulos. Un proyecto que, como el de publicar Platero y yo en cuadernos sueltos, no llegaría nunca a ver la luz.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"7483047631863016376\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer11390521885925479328">Born in Fuente Vaqueros, Granada, Spain, June 5 1898; died near Granada, August 19 1936, García Lorca is one of Spain's most deeply appreciated and highly revered poets and dramatists. His murder by the Nationalists at the start of the Spanish civil war brought sudden international fame, accompanied by an excess of political rhetoric which led a later generation to question his merits; after the i</span>
  <span id="freeText11390521885925479328" style="display:none">Born in Fuente Vaqueros, Granada, Spain, June 5 1898; died near Granada, August 19 1936, García Lorca is one of Spain's most deeply appreciated and highly revered poets and dramatists. His murder by the Nationalists at the start of the Spanish civil war brought sudden international fame, accompanied by an excess of political rhetoric which led a later generation to question his merits; after the inevitable slump, his reputation has recovered (largely with a shift in interest to the less obvious works). He must now be bracketed with Machado as one of the two greatest poets Spain has produced in the 20th century, and he is certainly Spain's greatest dramatist since the Golden Age. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11390521885925479328" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
